Framing the Issue of Non-Existent Things
The commentator frames the issue and states the known distinction of existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise nature of the dispute over whether non-existent things are distinct from one another.

Core Idea
The debate is not about non-existents in the mind, but about their distinction in themselves.

The commentator begins by stating the question: Are non-existent things distinct? He immediately clarifies that external existents are clearly distinct in the external world. This sets the stage for the real disagreement, which concerns non-existents and privations. The passage establishes the ground for the detailed argument to follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening question
Notice that the base text uses an interrogative form, indicating the issue is not settled absolutely but requires detailed treatment.
The commentator explains that this shows lack of certainty about either side on its own.
2
Identify the distinction of existents
The passage first asserts that external existents are distinct without confusion, and their distinction in themselves is beyond doubt.
This distinction is described as the difference between existence in itself and existence through forms.
3
Locate the point of disagreement
After discussing existents, the commentator turns to non-existents and states that there is disagreement about their distinction and the multiplicity required for it.
The disagreement is specifically about non-existents including privations, not about existents.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الذهني يدل على وجود الصور الجزئية ،ذهنا والصور الجزئية لا ترتسم في النفس المجردة، بل في المادي والمادي يقبل الحرارة والبرودة، ثم إن النفس قد يرتسم فيها ما تقبله كالغم والفرح ونظائرهما، والجواب عن الأول ظاهر لأن قابل الحرارة والبرودة هو الجسم الأعراض، وقوى النفس المدركة أعراض كما صرحوا به واعلم أن ها هنا مغالطة ذكرها الكاتبي في حكمة العين، بل اتخذها مذهباً لا بد من إيرادها وحلها وهي أن الموجود في الذهن موجود في الخارج البتة، لأن الذهن من الموجودات الخارجية والموجود في الموجود في الخارج موجود فيه، والجواب أن ما ذكره مبني على توهم فاسد وهو أن الخارج ظرف للذهن كالبيت للحقة والذهن ظرف للموجود الذهني، كالحقة للدرة فيلزم حينئذ مما ذكره ومنشؤه ملاحظة جانب اللفظ، واستعمال كلمة في الدالة على الظرفية وأما إذا حقق المعنى وعرف أن المراد بالوجود في الخارج هو الوجود الأصيلي الذي هو مصدر الآثار، ومظهر الأحكام وبالوجود الذهني هو الوجود الظلي الذي ليس كذلك، فيظهر سقوطه بالكلية أو لا يرى أنه إذا قيل: الموجود في الذهن موجود غير أصيلي، والذهن موجود بوجود أصيلي لم ينتظم الكلام . قوله : ( وتمايز الوجودات الخارجية بحسب أنفسها مما لا شك فيه أيضاً لأن الموجودات الخارجية إنما تتمايز ،باعتبارها وما به التمايز وإن لم يلزم أن يكون أمراً موجوداً ضرورة تميز بحسب الخارج متفرع على كونها موجودة فيه وأما المعدومات التي من جملتها العدمات ففي تمايزها وتعددها اللازم للتمايز خلاف ( منهم من أثبته فإن عدم الشرط عدم المشروط، وعدم الضد عن محل ( يصحح وجود الضد) الآخر فيه (دون غيرهما ) أي غير عدمي الشرط والضد فإن عدم غير الشرط لا يوجب عدم المشروط، النظر عن وجودها وعدمها مما لا شك فيه إذ لو كانت متحدة لكانت جميع الموجودات موجودة بوجود واحد وتمايزها في نفسها إنما يقتضي اتصاف الماهيات بها في نفس الأمر. قوله : ( وتمايزها بحسب (الخارج بأن تكون متصفة بالتمايز فيه متفرع على وجودها الخارج، لأن الاتصاف بالتمايز الخارجي بدون وجود الموصوف فيه محال، وأما تمايزها حال كونها معدومة فمتفرع على تمايز المعدومات، وبما حررنا لك ظهر اندفاع ما قيل : إن الوجودات الخارجية من المعدومات، فكيف لا يشك في تمايزها مع الشك في تمايز المعدومات؟ والحمل على أن الخلاف في تمايز سائر المعدومات يقتضي وجه الفرق قوله : (وأما المعدومات التي من جملتها العدمات أشار بإدخال العدمات التي هي من الممتنعات، إذ لو أمكن وجودها لأمكن وجود الممتنعات لاتصافها بها إلى أن هذه المسألة شاملة للمعدومات الممكنة والممتنعة وليست مختصة بالممكنة كمسألة الشيئية، وإلى أن العدمات من حيث كونها معدومة داخلة في هذه المسألة، وأما باعتبار إضافتها إلى ملكاتها فهي متمايزة بتمايز ملكاتها فإن قلت بعد القول بالمعدومات يصفة الجمع لا معنى للنزاع في تمايزها وهل ذلك إلا مثل أن يقال الأمور المتعددة هل هي متعددة متمايزة أو لا، وليس المراد التمايز في الخارج حتى يمكن أن يقال : المعدومات المتعددة في الذهن هل هي متمايزة في الخارج أولا قلت لا شبهة في تعددها من حيث الإضافة إلى الملكات كما عرفت، وذلك يكفي للتعبير بصيغة الجمع إنما النزاع في أن المعدومات المتعددة بحسب الإضافات هل هي متمايزة من حيث إنها متصفة بالعدم أم لا . قوله : (أي غير عدمي الشرط والضد لم يرجع الضمير إلى عدم المشروط ووجود الضد الآخر، فإنه يوجب اتصاف عدم الشرط بحكمين مختلفين والمطلوب اختلاف العدمين في الأحكام. قوله : ( فإن عدم غير الشرط إلخ ) أراد بالشرط ها هنا ما يتوقف عليه الشيء لا المعنى الأعمى [؟] بعماه عن غيره، لكن لا بد من تميزه في نفسه، وفيه بحث لأن الوجودات الخارجية من المعدومات، فكيف لا يشك في تمايزها ؟ مع الشك في تمايز المعدومات، والحمل على أن الخلاف في تمايز سائر المعدومات يقتضي وجه الفرق وبناء الكلام على وجود الوجود في الخارج بنفسه يدفعه قوله : وتمايزها بحسب الخارج إلخ كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( التي من جملتها العدمات) إشارة إلى تطبيق الدليل أعني قوله : فإن عدم الشرط إلخ على المدعي وهو تمايز المعدومات. قوله : (فإن عدم الشرط إلخ) اكتفى في الاستدلال بالتمثيلات إما لأن الدعوى مهملة وإثبات المهملة بالجزئيات منتظم، وإما بناء على أنه لا فرق بين الأعدام الخارجية في التمايز و عدمه ولا قائل بالفصل .
The First Observatory - The Fifth Objective: Are non-existent things distinct? (The external) because its source is concrete existence, so the essence of heat is impossible to occur in the mind, and it opposes the essence of cold, and the essence of the mountain is impossible to occur in the mind. So why did you say that mental [existence] is like that? This amount of summary answer suffices us, and we have no need for that particular detail concerning the existence of universals in the mind. The Fifth Objective: Are non-existent things distinct or not? (Are non-existents distinct? (or not? The external existents are distinct externally without confusion, and the distinction of external existents in themselves is also beyond doubt, and their distinction is the difference between two modes of occurrence: their occurrence in themselves and their occurrence through their forms. And he considered in the difference between them by imagining the disbelief of the disbeliever and its occurrence to the disbeliever, so the objection from the concomitants of quiddity does not apply, and likewise from mental concomitants like impossibility. His statement: (Are non-existents distinct? (or not) — he mentioned it with an interrogative to indicate lack of certainty about either side in an absolute sense, but rather according to the detail he mentioned in his statement 'and the truth'. His statement: (External existents, etc.) is a determination of the locus of dispute such that confusion is removed from it, and since things are clarified by their opposites, he embarked on explaining the distinction of existents and existences, so that it becomes clear that the disputed distinction of non-existents is in this sense. His statement: (And the distinction of existences) i.e., on the assumption that they are additional to quiddities in themselves, i.e., disregarding [?] heat and cold and their like, and the commentator also pointed to it in his glosses on al-Tajrid, and it was responded that the mentioned proof for mental existence indicates the existence of particular forms mentally, and particular forms are not imprinted in the abstract soul, but in the material, and the material receives heat and cold; moreover, the soul may have imprinted in it what it receives like grief and joy and their like. The answer to the first is obvious because the receiver of heat and cold is the body of accidents, and the faculties of the perceiving soul are accidents as they have stated. Know that here is a fallacy mentioned by al-Katibi in Hikmat al-Ayn, indeed he adopted it as a doctrine, and it must be presented and resolved: it is that what exists in the mind exists in the external world absolutely, because the mind is among the external existents, and what exists in what exists in the external world exists in it. The answer is that what he mentioned is based on a false illusion: that the external is a container for the mind like the house for the box, and the mind is a container for the mental existent like the box for the pearl; then what he mentioned follows, and its source is observing the verbal aspect and using the word 'fi' indicating containment. But if the meaning is verified and it is known that by 'existence in the external' is meant the principal existence which is the source of effects and manifestor of judgments, and by 'mental existence' is meant the shadow existence which is not so, then its fallaciousness becomes completely apparent, or else it is seen that if it is said: what exists in the mind is a non-principal existent, and the mind exists with a principal existence, the statement is not coherent. His statement: (And the distinction of external existents in themselves is also beyond doubt because external existents are distinguished by themselves, and that by which distinction occurs, even if it is not necessary that it be an existent thing, necessarily distinguishes according to the external, which branches from their being existent in it. As for non-existents, among which are privations, there is disagreement concerning their distinction and the multiplicity required for distinction. Some affirmed it: for the absence of a condition is the absence of the conditioned, and the absence of an opposite from a locus which renders possible the existence of the other opposite in it to the exclusion of the two others, i.e., other than the two absences of condition and opposite; for the absence of a non-condition does not necessitate the absence of the conditioned. Disregarding their existence and non-existence is beyond doubt, for if they were one, then all existents would exist with a single existence, and their distinction in themselves only requires the attribution of quiddities to them in themselves. His statement: (And their distinction according to the external, i.e., being characterized by distinction in it, is branched from their external existence, because characterization by external distinction without the existence of the characterized in it is impossible. As for their distinction when they are non-existent, it is branched from the distinction of non-existents. By what we have clarified for you, the refutation of what was said—that external existents are among non-existents, so how can one be certain of their distinction while doubting the distinction of non-existents? And the interpretation that the disagreement is concerning the distinction of other non-existents requires a differentiating factor—becomes evident. His statement: (And as for non-existents, among which are privations) he indicated by including privations—which are of the impossible things, for if their existence were possible then the existence of impossible things would be possible because they are characterized by them—that this issue includes possible and impossible non-existents and is not specific to possibles like the issue of thingness, and that privations insofar as they are non-existent are included in this issue. But with respect to their relation to their privations, they are distinguished by the distinction of their privations. If you say: after saying 'non-existents' in the plural form, there is no meaning to dispute about their distinction; is that not like saying: Are multiple things multiple and distinct or not? And it is not meant distinction in the external such that it could be said: Are the multiple non-existents in the mind distinct in the external or not? I say: There is no doubt about their multiplicity in terms of relation to the privations, as you know, and that suffices for the expression in the plural form. The dispute is only concerning whether the multiple non-existents in terms of relations are distinct insofar as they are characterized by non-existence or not. His statement: (i.e., other than the two absences of condition and opposite) the pronoun does not refer to the absence of the conditioned and the existence of the other opposite, for that would require the characterization of the absence of condition with two different judgments, while what is required is the difference of the two absences in judgments. His statement: (For the absence of a non-condition, etc.) he meant by 'condition' here that on which a thing depends, not the meaning 'blind' [؟] in its blindness to other than itself, but it must be distinct in itself. And there is discussion because external existents are among non-existents, so how is there no doubt about their distinction while doubting the distinction of non-existents? And the interpretation that the disagreement is concerning the distinction of other non-existents requires a differentiating factor, and the construction of the statement on the existence of existence in the external by itself is repelled by his statement: 'And their distinction according to the external, etc.' as is not hidden. His statement: (which are among privations) is an indication of applying the proof, i.e., his statement: 'For the absence of a condition, etc.' to the claim, which is the distinction of non-existents. His statement: (For the absence of a condition, etc.) he sufficed in inference with examples, either because the claim is indefinite and proving an indefinite with particulars is valid, or on the basis that there is no difference between external privations in distinction and non-distinction, and no one claims a distinction.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the fifth objective (al-maqsad al-khamis) of the first observation (al-marsad al-awwal) of the work. It addresses the question: Are non-existent things (al-ma'dumat) distinct from one another or not? The commentator begins by clarifying that the discussion concerns a specific sense of 'distinction' (tamayyuz). He first states that external existents (al-mawjudat al-kharijiyya) are distinct in the external world without confusion, and their distinction in themselves is beyond doubt. This distinction is described as the difference between two modes of occurrence: occurrence in themselves and occurrence through their forms. The commentator then explains that the base text uses an interrogative form to indicate that the issue is not settled absolutely, but rather requires detailed treatment. He then delves into a lengthy digression about mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni), refuting a fallacy attributed to al-Katibi. The fallacy is that since the mind is an external existent, whatever exists in the mind must also exist in the external world. The commentator refutes this by distinguishing between 'principal existence' (al-wujud al-asili) and 'shadow existence' (al-wujud al-zilli), showing that the mental existence is only a shadow and not productive of external effects. After this digression, the commentator returns to the main topic: non-existents, including privations (al-'adamat). He explains that there is disagreement about their distinction and the multiplicity required for distinction. Some affirm distinction using examples: the absence of a condition (adam al-shart) results in the absence of the conditioned, and the absence of an opposite (adam al-didd) from a locus makes possible the existence of the other opposite. The commentator clarifies that these examples show that different absences have different effects, implying their distinction. He then addresses an objection: if external existents are themselves non-existent in a certain sense, how can we be certain of their distinction while doubting the distinction of other non-existents? The answer is that the disagreement is only about non-existents other than external existents. The commentator further explains that the inclusion of privations indicates that the issue covers both possible and impossible non-existents. He also discusses the meaning of 'multiple' non-existents: the plurality comes from their relation to their privations (malakat), and the dispute is whether they are distinct in themselves as characterized by non-existence. Finally, he comments on the proof's structure, noting that the examples suffice because the claim is indefinite and proving an indefinite with particulars is valid, or because there is no difference among external privations regarding distinction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the debate is about whether non-existents have distinct concepts in the mind.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the dispute is about distinction in themselves as characterized by non-existence, not merely in mental concepts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of external existents regarding distinction?
A) They are distinct in the external world but not in themselves.
B) They are distinct in the external world and their distinction in themselves is beyond doubt.
C) They are not distinct because they are non-existent.
D) Their distinction is disputed.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over the distinction of non-existents is not about existents, which are clearly distinct, but about whether non-existent things are distinct in themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine the disbelief of a disbeliever, you have a clear concept in your mind. But does your own faith exist merely as a concept in your mind, or is it actualized in your life? What one external action today can prove that your faith is not just a mental form but a reality in yourself?
?
The passage distinguishes between 'occurrence in themselves' and 'occurrence through forms'. Are there any truths about Allah or the unseen that you only 'know' through forms (words, concepts) but have not yet made them occur in your heart with certainty? What would it take for that knowledge to become a lived reality in your actions?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
8 min
The Proof: Absence of Condition and Opposite
The commentator provides the proof using two examples of absences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key argument used to prove that non-existent things are distinct through the examples of absence of a condition and absence of an opposite.

Core Idea
Different absences produce different effects, proving they are distinct.

After clarifying the locus of dispute, the commentator presents a proof for the distinction of non-existents. The proof uses two examples: the absence of a condition and the absence of an opposite. These examples show that different absences lead to different consequences, which implies that the absences themselves are distinct. This is a concrete argument grounded in observable effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Find the sentence that begins 'فإن عدم الشرط عدم المشروط' and read it carefully. This is the core of the proof.
2
Identify the two examples
The proof mentions two specific types of absence: absence of a condition and absence of an opposite. Each has a different effect.
Absence of condition results in absence of the conditioned; absence of an opposite from a locus enables the opposite to exist.
3
Understand the implication
If these absences were not distinct, they would have the same effects. But they have different effects, so they must be distinct.
This shows that non-existents can be distinguished by their causal roles.

حررنا لك ظهر اندفاع ما قيل : إن الوجودات الخارجية من المعدومات، فكيف لا يشك في تمايزها مع الشك في تمايز المعدومات؟ والحمل على أن الخلاف في تمايز سائر المعدومات يقتضي وجه الفرق قوله : (وأما المعدومات التي من جملتها العدمات أشار بإدخال العدمات التي هي من الممتنعات، إذ لو أمكن وجودها لأمكن وجود الممتنعات لاتصافها بها إلى أن هذه المسألة شاملة للمعدومات الممكنة والممتنعة وليست مختصة بالممكنة كمسألة الشيئية، وإلى أن العدمات من حيث كونها معدومة داخلة في هذه المسألة، وأما باعتبار إضافتها إلى ملكاتها فهي متمايزة بتمايز ملكاتها فإن قلت بعد القول بالمعدومات يصفة الجمع لا معنى للنزاع في تمايزها وهل ذلك إلا مثل أن يقال الأمور المتعددة هل هي متعددة متمايزة أو لا، وليس المراد التمايز في الخارج حتى يمكن أن يقال : المعدومات المتعددة في الذهن هل هي متمايزة في الخارج أولا قلت لا شبهة في تعددها من حيث الإضافة إلى الملكات كما عرفت، وذلك يكفي للتعبير بصيغة الجمع إنما النزاع في أن المعدومات المتعددة بحسب الإضافات هل هي متمايزة من حيث إنها متصفة بالعدم أم لا . قوله : (أي غير عدمي الشرط والضد لم يرجع الضمير إلى عدم المشروط ووجود الضد الآخر، فإنه يوجب اتصاف عدم الشرط بحكمين مختلفين والمطلوب اختلاف العدمين في الأحكام. قوله : ( فإن عدم غير الشرط إلخ ) أراد بالشرط ها هنا ما يتوقف عليه الشيء لا المعنى الأعمى [؟] بعماه عن غيره، لكن لا بد من تميزه في نفسه، وفيه بحث لأن الوجودات الخارجية من المعدومات، فكيف لا يشك في تمايزها ؟ مع الشك في تمايز المعدومات، والحمل على أن الخلاف في تمايز سائر المعدومات يقتضي وجه الفرق وبناء الكلام على وجود الوجود في الخارج بنفسه يدفعه قوله : وتمايزها بحسب الخارج إلخ كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( التي من جملتها العدمات) إشارة إلى تطبيق الدليل أعني قوله : فإن عدم الشرط إلخ على المدعي وهو تمايز المعدومات. قوله : (فإن عدم الشرط إلخ) اكتفى في الاستدلال بالتمثيلات إما لأن الدعوى مهملة وإثبات المهملة بالجزئيات منتظم، وإما بناء على أنه لا فرق بين الأعدام الخارجية في التمايز و عدمه ولا قائل بالفصل .
The First Observatory - The Fifth Objective: Are non-existent things distinct? (The external) because its source is concrete existence, so the essence of heat is impossible to occur in the mind, and it opposes the essence of cold, and the essence of the mountain is impossible to occur in the mind. So why did you say that mental [existence] is like that? This amount of summary answer suffices us, and we have no need for that particular detail concerning the existence of universals in the mind. The Fifth Objective: Are non-existent things distinct or not? (Are non-existents distinct? (or not? The external existents are distinct externally without confusion, and the distinction of external existents in themselves is also beyond doubt, and their distinction is the difference between two modes of occurrence: their occurrence in themselves and their occurrence through their forms. And he considered in the difference between them by imagining the disbelief of the disbeliever and its occurrence to the disbeliever, so the objection from the concomitants of quiddity does not apply, and likewise from mental concomitants like impossibility. His statement: (Are non-existents distinct? (or not) — he mentioned it with an interrogative to indicate lack of certainty about either side in an absolute sense, but rather according to the detail he mentioned in his statement 'and the truth'. His statement: (External existents, etc.) is a determination of the locus of dispute such that confusion is removed from it, and since things are clarified by their opposites, he embarked on explaining the distinction of existents and existences, so that it becomes clear that the disputed distinction of non-existents is in this sense. His statement: (And the distinction of existences) i.e., on the assumption that they are additional to quiddities in themselves, i.e., disregarding [?] heat and cold and their like, and the commentator also pointed to it in his glosses on al-Tajrid, and it was responded that the mentioned proof for mental existence indicates the existence of particular forms mentally, and particular forms are not imprinted in the abstract soul, but in the material, and the material receives heat and cold; moreover, the soul may have imprinted in it what it receives like grief and joy and their like. The answer to the first is obvious because the receiver of heat and cold is the body of accidents, and the faculties of the perceiving soul are accidents as they have stated. Know that here is a fallacy mentioned by al-Katibi in Hikmat al-Ayn, indeed he adopted it as a doctrine, and it must be presented and resolved: it is that what exists in the mind exists in the external world absolutely, because the mind is among the external existents, and what exists in what exists in the external world exists in it. The answer is that what he mentioned is based on a false illusion: that the external is a container for the mind like the house for the box, and the mind is a container for the mental existent like the box for the pearl; then what he mentioned follows, and its source is observing the verbal aspect and using the word 'fi' indicating containment. But if the meaning is verified and it is known that by 'existence in the external' is meant the principal existence which is the source of effects and manifestor of judgments, and by 'mental existence' is meant the shadow existence which is not so, then its fallaciousness becomes completely apparent, or else it is seen that if it is said: what exists in the mind is a non-principal existent, and the mind exists with a principal existence, the statement is not coherent. His statement: (And the distinction of external existents in themselves is also beyond doubt because external existents are distinguished by themselves, and that by which distinction occurs, even if it is not necessary that it be an existent thing, necessarily distinguishes according to the external, which branches from their being existent in it. As for non-existents, among which are privations, there is disagreement concerning their distinction and the multiplicity required for distinction. Some affirmed it: for the absence of a condition is the absence of the conditioned, and the absence of an opposite from a locus which renders possible the existence of the other opposite in it to the exclusion of the two others, i.e., other than the two absences of condition and opposite; for the absence of a non-condition does not necessitate the absence of the conditioned. Disregarding their existence and non-existence is beyond doubt, for if they were one, then all existents would exist with a single existence, and their distinction in themselves only requires the attribution of quiddities to them in themselves. His statement: (And their distinction according to the external, i.e., being characterized by distinction in it, is branched from their external existence, because characterization by external distinction without the existence of the characterized in it is impossible. As for their distinction when they are non-existent, it is branched from the distinction of non-existents. By what we have clarified for you, the refutation of what was said—that external existents are among non-existents, so how can one be certain of their distinction while doubting the distinction of non-existents? And the interpretation that the disagreement is concerning the distinction of other non-existents requires a differentiating factor—becomes evident. His statement: (And as for non-existents, among which are privations) he indicated by including privations—which are of the impossible things, for if their existence were possible then the existence of impossible things would be possible because they are characterized by them—that this issue includes possible and impossible non-existents and is not specific to possibles like the issue of thingness, and that privations insofar as they are non-existent are included in this issue. But with respect to their relation to their privations, they are distinguished by the distinction of their privations. If you say: after saying 'non-existents' in the plural form, there is no meaning to dispute about their distinction; is that not like saying: Are multiple things multiple and distinct or not? And it is not meant distinction in the external such that it could be said: Are the multiple non-existents in the mind distinct in the external or not? I say: There is no doubt about their multiplicity in terms of relation to the privations, as you know, and that suffices for the expression in the plural form. The dispute is only concerning whether the multiple non-existents in terms of relations are distinct insofar as they are characterized by non-existence or not. His statement: (i.e., other than the two absences of condition and opposite) the pronoun does not refer to the absence of the conditioned and the existence of the other opposite, for that would require the characterization of the absence of condition with two different judgments, while what is required is the difference of the two absences in judgments. His statement: (For the absence of a non-condition, etc.) he meant by 'condition' here that on which a thing depends, not the meaning 'blind' [؟] in its blindness to other than itself, but it must be distinct in itself. And there is discussion because external existents are among non-existents, so how is there no doubt about their distinction while doubting the distinction of non-existents? And the interpretation that the disagreement is concerning the distinction of other non-existents requires a differentiating factor, and the construction of the statement on the existence of existence in the external by itself is repelled by his statement: 'And their distinction according to the external, etc.' as is not hidden. His statement: (which are among privations) is an indication of applying the proof, i.e., his statement: 'For the absence of a condition, etc.' to the claim, which is the distinction of non-existents. His statement: (For the absence of a condition, etc.) he sufficed in inference with examples, either because the claim is indefinite and proving an indefinite with particulars is valid, or on the basis that there is no difference between external privations in distinction and non-distinction, and no one claims a distinction.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the fifth objective (al-maqsad al-khamis) of the first observation (al-marsad al-awwal) of the work. It addresses the question: Are non-existent things (al-ma'dumat) distinct from one another or not? The commentator begins by clarifying that the discussion concerns a specific sense of 'distinction' (tamayyuz). He first states that external existents (al-mawjudat al-kharijiyya) are distinct in the external world without confusion, and their distinction in themselves is beyond doubt. This distinction is described as the difference between two modes of occurrence: occurrence in themselves and occurrence through their forms. The commentator then explains that the base text uses an interrogative form to indicate that the issue is not settled absolutely, but rather requires detailed treatment. He then delves into a lengthy digression about mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni), refuting a fallacy attributed to al-Katibi. The fallacy is that since the mind is an external existent, whatever exists in the mind must also exist in the external world. The commentator refutes this by distinguishing between 'principal existence' (al-wujud al-asili) and 'shadow existence' (al-wujud al-zilli), showing that the mental existence is only a shadow and not productive of external effects. After this digression, the commentator returns to the main topic: non-existents, including privations (al-'adamat). He explains that there is disagreement about their distinction and the multiplicity required for distinction. Some affirm distinction using examples: the absence of a condition (adam al-shart) results in the absence of the conditioned, and the absence of an opposite (adam al-didd) from a locus makes possible the existence of the other opposite. The commentator clarifies that these examples show that different absences have different effects, implying their distinction. He then addresses an objection: if external existents are themselves non-existent in a certain sense, how can we be certain of their distinction while doubting the distinction of other non-existents? The answer is that the disagreement is only about non-existents other than external existents. The commentator further explains that the inclusion of privations indicates that the issue covers both possible and impossible non-existents. He also discusses the meaning of 'multiple' non-existents: the plurality comes from their relation to their privations (malakat), and the dispute is whether they are distinct in themselves as characterized by non-existence. Finally, he comments on the proof's structure, noting that the examples suffice because the claim is indefinite and proving an indefinite with particulars is valid, or because there is no difference among external privations regarding distinction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the absence of a condition (adam al-shart) result in according to the proof?
A) The existence of the opposite.
B) The absence of the conditioned.
C) The possibility of the conditioned.
D) The existence of the condition.

Key Takeaway
The distinction of non-existents is evidenced by the fact that different absences produce different effects, such as the absence of condition vs. absence of opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that the absence of a condition (like sincere intention) results in the absence of its effect (accepted deeds). Think of one action you did today—was the condition of sincerity truly present? If not, then that action is void in the Sight of Allah. What specific step will you take tomorrow to ensure that condition is met?
?
Consider an absence of an opposite in your heart: where humility is absent, arrogance dwells. Right now, is there a relationship, a habit, or a thought where the absence of humility allows arrogance to reign? Can you name it? What would it cost you to bring humility back—and are you willing to pay that price?
?
The proof shows that different absences cause different realities. Reflect on the absence of istighfār (seeking forgiveness) in your daily routine: its absence leads to the accumulation of sins. Are you consciously cultivating the presence of istighfār, or are you letting its absence go unnoticed? Commit to a specific time today to say 'Astaghfirullāh' with presence of heart.

Rational Judgments
advanced
12 min
Refuting the Fallacy of Mental Existence
The commentator presents and refutes a fallacy about mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fallacy that whatever exists in the mind must exist externally, and the correct distinction between principal and shadow existence.

Core Idea
Mental existence is a shadow, not a principal existence producing external effects.

The commentator addresses a fallacy raised by al-Katibi in Hikmat al-Ayn. The fallacy claims that since the mind is an external existent, anything existing in the mind must also exist externally. This would blur the distinction between mental and external existence. The commentator refutes this by distinguishing between principal existence (al-wujud al-asili) and shadow existence (al-wujud al-zilli), showing that mental existence is only a shadow and not productive of external effects.

Guided Path
1
Understand the fallacy
Al-Katibi's argument: the mind is an external existent; therefore, whatever is in the mind is in an external existent, so it exists externally.
This is based on a spatial interpretation of 'in' (fi).
2
Identify the refutation
The commentator points out the false assumption that the external is a container for the mind like a house for a box, and the mind is a container for mental images.
He then clarifies the real meanings of 'existence in the external' and 'mental existence'.
3
Grasp the key distinction
Principal existence (al-wujud al-asili) is the source of effects and judgments, while mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni) is a shadow existence that does not produce external effects.
Thus, something can exist in the mind without having principal existence in the external world.

The First Observatory - The Fifth Objective: Are non-existent things distinct? (The external) because its source is concrete existence, so the essence of heat is impossible to occur in the mind, and it opposes the essence of cold, and the essence of the mountain is impossible to occur in the mind. So why did you say that mental [existence] is like that? This amount of summary answer suffices us, and we have no need for that particular detail concerning the existence of universals in the mind. The Fifth Objective: Are non-existent things distinct or not? (Are non-existents distinct? (or not? The external existents are distinct externally without confusion, and the distinction of external existents in themselves is also beyond doubt, and their distinction is the difference between two modes of occurrence: their occurrence in themselves and their occurrence through their forms. And he considered in the difference between them by imagining the disbelief of the disbeliever and its occurrence to the disbeliever, so the objection from the concomitants of quiddity does not apply, and likewise from mental concomitants like impossibility. His statement: (Are non-existents distinct? (or not) — he mentioned it with an interrogative to indicate lack of certainty about either side in an absolute sense, but rather according to the detail he mentioned in his statement 'and the truth'. His statement: (External existents, etc.) is a determination of the locus of dispute such that confusion is removed from it, and since things are clarified by their opposites, he embarked on explaining the distinction of existents and existences, so that it becomes clear that the disputed distinction of non-existents is in this sense. His statement: (And the distinction of existences) i.e., on the assumption that they are additional to quiddities in themselves, i.e., disregarding [?] heat and cold and their like, and the commentator also pointed to it in his glosses on al-Tajrid, and it was responded that the mentioned proof for mental existence indicates the existence of particular forms mentally, and particular forms are not imprinted in the abstract soul, but in the material, and the material receives heat and cold; moreover, the soul may have imprinted in it what it receives like grief and joy and their like. The answer to the first is obvious because the receiver of heat and cold is the body of accidents, and the faculties of the perceiving soul are accidents as they have stated. Know that here is a fallacy mentioned by al-Katibi in Hikmat al-Ayn, indeed he adopted it as a doctrine, and it must be presented and resolved: it is that what exists in the mind exists in the external world absolutely, because the mind is among the external existents, and what exists in what exists in the external world exists in it. The answer is that what he mentioned is based on a false illusion: that the external is a container for the mind like the house for the box, and the mind is a container for the mental existent like the box for the pearl; then what he mentioned follows, and its source is observing the verbal aspect and using the word 'fi' indicating containment. But if the meaning is verified and it is known that by 'existence in the external' is meant the principal existence which is the source of effects and manifestor of judgments, and by 'mental existence' is meant the shadow existence which is not so, then its fallaciousness becomes completely apparent, or else it is seen that if it is said: what exists in the mind is a non-principal existent, and the mind exists with a principal existence, the statement is not coherent. His statement: (And the distinction of external existents in themselves is also beyond doubt because external existents are distinguished by themselves, and that by which distinction occurs, even if it is not necessary that it be an existent thing, necessarily distinguishes according to the external, which branches from their being existent in it. As for non-existents, among which are privations, there is disagreement concerning their distinction and the multiplicity required for distinction. Some affirmed it: for the absence of a condition is the absence of the conditioned, and the absence of an opposite from a locus which renders possible the existence of the other opposite in it to the exclusion of the two others, i.e., other than the two absences of condition and opposite; for the absence of a non-condition does not necessitate the absence of the conditioned. Disregarding their existence and non-existence is beyond doubt, for if they were one, then all existents would exist with a single existence, and their distinction in themselves only requires the attribution of quiddities to them in themselves. His statement: (And their distinction according to the external, i.e., being characterized by distinction in it, is branched from their external existence, because characterization by external distinction without the existence of the characterized in it is impossible. As for their distinction when they are non-existent, it is branched from the distinction of non-existents. By what we have clarified for you, the refutation of what was said—that external existents are among non-existents, so how can one be certain of their distinction while doubting the distinction of non-existents? And the interpretation that the disagreement is concerning the distinction of other non-existents requires a differentiating factor—becomes evident. His statement: (And as for non-existents, among which are privations) he indicated by including privations—which are of the impossible things, for if their existence were possible then the existence of impossible things would be possible because they are characterized by them—that this issue includes possible and impossible non-existents and is not specific to possibles like the issue of thingness, and that privations insofar as they are non-existent are included in this issue. But with respect to their relation to their privations, they are distinguished by the distinction of their privations. If you say: after saying 'non-existents' in the plural form, there is no meaning to dispute about their distinction; is that not like saying: Are multiple things multiple and distinct or not? And it is not meant distinction in the external such that it could be said: Are the multiple non-existents in the mind distinct in the external or not? I say: There is no doubt about their multiplicity in terms of relation to the privations, as you know, and that suffices for the expression in the plural form. The dispute is only concerning whether the multiple non-existents in terms of relations are distinct insofar as they are characterized by non-existence or not. His statement: (i.e., other than the two absences of condition and opposite) the pronoun does not refer to the absence of the conditioned and the existence of the other opposite, for that would require the characterization of the absence of condition with two different judgments, while what is required is the difference of the two absences in judgments. His statement: (For the absence of a non-condition, etc.) he meant by 'condition' here that on which a thing depends, not the meaning 'blind' [؟] in its blindness to other than itself, but it must be distinct in itself. And there is discussion because external existents are among non-existents, so how is there no doubt about their distinction while doubting the distinction of non-existents? And the interpretation that the disagreement is concerning the distinction of other non-existents requires a differentiating factor, and the construction of the statement on the existence of existence in the external by itself is repelled by his statement: 'And their distinction according to the external, etc.' as is not hidden. His statement: (which are among privations) is an indication of applying the proof, i.e., his statement: 'For the absence of a condition, etc.' to the claim, which is the distinction of non-existents. His statement: (For the absence of a condition, etc.) he sufficed in inference with examples, either because the claim is indefinite and proving an indefinite with particulars is valid, or on the basis that there is no difference between external privations in distinction and non-distinction, and no one claims a distinction.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the fifth objective (al-maqsad al-khamis) of the first observation (al-marsad al-awwal) of the work. It addresses the question: Are non-existent things (al-ma'dumat) distinct from one another or not? The commentator begins by clarifying that the discussion concerns a specific sense of 'distinction' (tamayyuz). He first states that external existents (al-mawjudat al-kharijiyya) are distinct in the external world without confusion, and their distinction in themselves is beyond doubt. This distinction is described as the difference between two modes of occurrence: occurrence in themselves and occurrence through their forms. The commentator then explains that the base text uses an interrogative form to indicate that the issue is not settled absolutely, but rather requires detailed treatment. He then delves into a lengthy digression about mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni), refuting a fallacy attributed to al-Katibi. The fallacy is that since the mind is an external existent, whatever exists in the mind must also exist in the external world. The commentator refutes this by distinguishing between 'principal existence' (al-wujud al-asili) and 'shadow existence' (al-wujud al-zilli), showing that the mental existence is only a shadow and not productive of external effects. After this digression, the commentator returns to the main topic: non-existents, including privations (al-'adamat). He explains that there is disagreement about their distinction and the multiplicity required for distinction. Some affirm distinction using examples: the absence of a condition (adam al-shart) results in the absence of the conditioned, and the absence of an opposite (adam al-didd) from a locus makes possible the existence of the other opposite. The commentator clarifies that these examples show that different absences have different effects, implying their distinction. He then addresses an objection: if external existents are themselves non-existent in a certain sense, how can we be certain of their distinction while doubting the distinction of other non-existents? The answer is that the disagreement is only about non-existents other than external existents. The commentator further explains that the inclusion of privations indicates that the issue covers both possible and impossible non-existents. He also discusses the meaning of 'multiple' non-existents: the plurality comes from their relation to their privations (malakat), and the dispute is whether they are distinct in themselves as characterized by non-existence. Finally, he comments on the proof's structure, noting that the examples suffice because the claim is indefinite and proving an indefinite with particulars is valid, or because there is no difference among external privations regarding distinction.

Key Terms

وجود أصيلي
وجود ظلي
مغالطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something exists in the mind, it must exist in the external world in the same way.
Correction: Mental existence is only a shadow (zilli) and lacks the effects of principal (asili) existence, so it does not entail external existence.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of al-Katibi's fallacy according to the commentator?
A) The assumption that the mind is not an external existent.
B) The false illusion that the external is a container for the mind like a house for a box.
C) The belief that mental existence is the same as principal existence.
D) The idea that 'existence' is univocal.

Key Takeaway
Mental existence is a shadow of principal existence, so an object in the mind does not have the effects of an external object, refuting the fallacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The fallacy arose from a careless use of the word 'fi' (in). What is one belief you hold that might be based on a similar confusion—taking a mental concept as if it were a real, effective truth? Will you test it this week?
?
Imagine you have an idea you are passionate about. How can you distinguish whether your passion is for a shadow (mental existence) or for the reality (principal existence)? What steps will you take to ground your conviction in reality?
?
Al-Katibi adopted this fallacy as a doctrine. Is there a belief you hold that, if examined, might rely on a mental construct rather than actual truth? What would it cost you to abandon that construct, and why haven't you done so yet?

First Opinion on Distinction of Non-Existents and Its Proof
Presents the first opinion affirming distinction of non-existents with proof from condition and opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first opinion affirms the distinction of non-existents and the proof it uses based on differential effects such as condition and opposite.

Core Idea
Non-existents are distinguished by their effects, not by existence.

This passage dives into a subtle debate in kalam: whether non-existents can be said to be distinct from each other. The author presents the first opinion, which affirms distinction, and supports it with a clever proof: different non-existences have different consequences in reality. Understanding this argument requires careful attention to the concepts of condition, opposite, and their non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Focus on the flow from the issue to the proof to the objection.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the core issue
Identify what is being debated: do non-existents (al-ma'dūmāt) differ from each other? Note that this is separate from their status in external existence.
The issue is about non-existents qua non-existent, not as existent.
3
Analyze the proof
Find the proof: the non-existence of the condition leads to non-existence of the conditioned, but non-existence of a non-condition does not. Similarly for opposite. This shows different non-existences have different effects, implying they are distinct.
Think about examples: e.g., the non-existence of life leads to non-existence of knowledge, but not vice versa.
4
Examine the refutation and scope
Read how the author addresses the objection that external existences are themselves non-existent. Understand the clarification that the dispute is about non-existents other than those that are actually existent.
The inclusion of 'adamāt (non-existences) covers impossible non-existents as well.
5
Connect to key terms
Review the key terms (al-ma'dūmāt, al-'adamāt, al-tamāyuz, etc.) and see how they are used in the argument.
These terms are central to the discussion.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الخامس : المعدومات هل تتمايز أم لا يوجب بحسب الخارج متفرع على كونها موجودة فيه وأما المعدومات التي من جملتها العدمات ففي تمايزها وتعددها اللازم للتمايز خلاف ( منهم من أثبته فإن عدم الشرط عدم المشروط، وعدم الضد عن محل ( يصحح وجود الضد) الآخر فيه (دون غيرهما ) أي غير عدمي الشرط والضد فإن عدم غير الشرط لا يوجب عدم المشروط . قوله : ( وتمايزها بحسب (الخارج بأن تكون متصفة بالتمايز فيه متفرع على وجودها الخارج، لأن الاتصاف بالتمايز الخارجي بدون وجود الموصوف فيه محال، وأما تمايزها حال كونها معدومة فمتفرع على تمايز المعدومات، وبما حررنا لك ظهر اندفاع ما قيل : إن الوجودات الخارجية من المعدومات، فكيف لا يشك في تمايزها مع الشك في تمايز المعدومات؟ والحمل على أن الخلاف في تمايز سائر المعدومات يقتضي وجه الفرق . قوله : (وأما المعدومات التي من جملتها العدمات أشار بإدخال العدمات التي هي من الممتنعات، إذ لو أمكن وجودها لأمكن وجود الممتنعات لاتصافها بها إلى أن هذه المسألة شاملة للمعدومات الممكنة والممتنعة وليست مختصة بالممكنة كمسألة الشيئية، وإلى أن العدمات من حيث كونها معدومة داخلة في هذه المسألة، وأما باعتبار إضافتها إلى ملكاتها فهي متمايزة بتمايز ملكاتها
The first observation - the fifth chapter: Do non-existents (al-ma'dūmāt) differ from each other or not. It necessitates according to the external (world) branching from their being existent in it. As for non-existents which include the non-existences (al-'adamāt), concerning their differentiation (tamāyuz) and multiplicity (ta'addud) required for differentiation, there is a dispute. Some affirm it: indeed, the non-existence of the condition is the non-existence of the conditioned, and the non-existence of the opposite in a locus that makes the existence of the other opposite possible in it, to the exclusion of other than them, i.e., other than the non-existences of the condition and the opposite, for the non-existence of other than the condition does not necessitate the non-existence of the conditioned. His saying: 'And their differentiation according to the external (world) is branching from their being existent in it' because being characterized by external differentiation without existence of the characterized (thing) in it is impossible. As for their differentiation in the state of their being non-existent, it branches from the differentiation of non-existents. And by what we have clarified for you, the refutation of what was said appears: 'the external existences are among non-existents, so why is there no doubt about their differentiation while there is doubt about the differentiation of non-existents?' And the interpretation that the dispute is about the differentiation of other non-existents would require a distinction. His saying: 'As for the non-existents which include the non-existences' he indicated by including the non-existences which are among the impossible (al-mumtani'āt), for if their existence were possible, the existence of impossibilities would be possible due to their being characterized by them, that this issue encompasses possible and impossible non-existents and is not specific to possibilities like the issue of thingness (shay'iyya), and that the non-existences in terms of their being non-existent are included in this issue; but in terms of their addition (iḍāfa) to their privations (malakāt), they are differentiated by the differentiation of their privations.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents the issue of whether non-existents (al-ma'dūmāt) are distinct from each other. It opens by noting that external differentiation requires existence, but the real question concerns non-existents themselves, including pure non-existences (al-'adamāt). The author then reports that some scholars affirm distinction and gives a proof: the non-existence of the condition is the non-existence of the conditioned, and the non-existence of one opposite in a locus that could contain the other opposite is specific to that opposite alone. This shows that different non-existences have different effects, implying they are distinct. The commentator explains that external differentiation depends on existence, but the differentiation of non-existents in their state of non-existence branches from the differentiation of the non-existents themselves. He refutes an objection that external existences are themselves non-existents, clarifying that the dispute is about other non-existents. He also explains that including 'adamāt (non-existences) covers impossible non-existents, and that these are distinguished by their relation to their privations (malakāt).

Key Takeaway
Non-existents are distinguished by their differential effects, not by existence in the external world.

If that condition is absent, what is missing in your heart? Are you treating that absence as trivial, or do you see its distinct consequence? What one step will you take to establish that condition in your life?

Objection and Answer on the Plural of Non-Existents
Objection and answer about the plural form of non-existents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the plural 'non-existents' is used despite the real dispute being about their intrinsic distinctness, not mere multiplicity.

Core Idea
The plural 'non-existents' is uncontroversial; the real debate is about their intrinsic distinction.

The objection attempts to dissolve the debate by claiming that once we admit the plural form, the argument is over. But as Sanusi shows, the plural is used because of relation to privations, whereas the dispute concerns whether non-existents considered purely as non-existent are distinct. This passage clarifies the precise point of contention.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection: 'If you say: After saying "non-existents" in the plural form, there is no meaning to dispute about their differentiation. Is this not like saying: The multiple things, are they multiple and distinct or not?' Understand that the objector thinks the plural form already settles the issue.
Notice the analogy: asking whether multiple things are multiple is trivial.
2
Understand the clarification about external vs mental
The objector adds that the intended differentiation is not in the external world, so the dispute would be meaningless. Recognize that this misconstrues the actual debate.
The objector assumes the only possible differentiation is external, but the disputants mean intrinsic distinction as non-existent.
3
Grasp the answer's distinction
The answer concedes multiplicity by relation to privations but insists the dispute is about whether non-existents are distinct in terms of being characterized by non-existence itself. That is the real point.
The plural form is not the issue; the issue is whether non-existents are intrinsically distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت بعد القول بالمعدومات يصفة الجمع لا معنى للنزاع في تمايزها وهل ذلك إلا مثل أن يقال الأمور المتعددة هل هي متعددة متمايزة أو لا، وليس المراد التمايز في الخارج حتى يمكن أن يقال : المعدومات المتعددة في الذهن هل هي متمايزة في الخارج أولا قلت لا شبهة في تعددها من حيث الإضافة إلى الملكات كما عرفت، وذلك يكفي للتعبير بصيغة الجمع إنما النزاع في أن المعدومات المتعددة بحسب الإضافات هل هي متمايزة من حيث إنها متصفة بالعدم أم لا .
If you say: After saying 'non-existents' in the plural form, there is no meaning to dispute about their differentiation. Is this not like saying: The multiple things, are they multiple and distinct or not? And the intended meaning is not differentiation in the external such that it could be said: The multiple non-existents in the mind, are they distinct in the external or not? I say: There is no doubt about their multiplicity in terms of addition to their privations as you know, and that suffices for expression in the plural form. The dispute is only about whether the multiple non-existents according to the additions are distinct in terms of being characterized by non-existence or not.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector argues that if non-existents are multiple only by relation to their privations (malakāt), then their multiplicity is already established, so why dispute their distinction? That would be like asking whether multiple things are multiple. The answer clarifies that the multiplicity by relation to privations is not what is disputed; that is agreed upon. Rather, the genuine point of contention is whether these same non-existents, considered purely as characterized by non-existence (and not by their relations), are intrinsically distinct from each other. Thus the plural form is used because of their relational multiplicity, but the argument concerns their intrinsic distinctness.

The Rational Proof Against Distinction of Non-Existents
Presents the second opinion that denies any distinction among non-existents with a formal logical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second opinion that denies any distinction among non-existents and the logical syllogism supporting it.

Core Idea
Non-existents cannot be distinct because distinction requires existence.

The first opinion held that non-existents are distinct due to essential differences. The second opinion, presented here, argues that non-existents are pure negation with no reference, and therefore cannot be distinct. The argument is built on a clear syllogism that the commentator elaborates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the flow from the conditional remark to the main proof.
The passage includes both the matn and the commentator's explanation.
2
Identify the two parts of the proof
First, there is a conditional (if non-existents were distinct, their implications would differ) which is not fully expanded. Second, the main argument using pure negation and the positive attribute of distinction.
The conditional serves as a prelude to the main syllogism.
3
Understand the syllogism
The commentator formalizes the proof: minor premise (non-existents have no existence), major premise (everything distinct exists in some manner), conclusion (no non-existent is distinct). Recognize why each premise is considered self-evident.
The minor premise is self-evident because non-existence contradicts subsistence; the major premise is justified because distinction is a positive attribute.
4
Reflect on the self-evident nature
Note that the premises are claimed to be badīhiyya (self-evident) in kalam reasoning, meaning they do not require further proof.
This addresses the objection of circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فله وجود وعدم غير الضد لا يصحح وجود الضد الآخر ( ولولا التمايز) والتعدد اللازم منه بين العدمات لم تختلف مقتضياتها ولا أحكامها من كون بعضها ملزوماً لآخر، أو لازماً له أو مساوياً أو مبايناً إلى غير ذلك مما لا كثرة ( ومنهم من نفاه لأن المعدومات) والعدمات (نفي صرف لا إشارة إليها أصلا، وكل ما هو متميز إما في الذهن وإما في الخارج لأن التميز صفة ،ثبوتية لا بد أن يكون الموصوف بها في الجملة، وما يكون ثابتاً كذلك لم يكن معدوماً، فلا شيء من المعدوم بمتميز أصلاً . قوله : ( لأن المعدومات إلخ) أي من حيث إنها معدومات نفي صرف أي خالص لا إشارة إليها، إذ الإشارة تقتضي الهوية عند العقل المنافية لكونها معدومة وهذه المقدمة بديهية، فلا يرد أن قولكم نفي صرف لا إشارة إليها بمنزلة أنه لا تميز لها، فيكون مصادرة وصورة الاستدلال أن المعدومات من حيث إنها معدومة لا وجود لها أصلاً، وكل ما هو متميز موجود في الجملة أما الصغرى فبديهية لأن العدم ينافي الثبوت، وأما الكبرى فلان كل متميز متصف بالصفة الثبوتية التميز، وكل متصف بالصفة الثبوتية موجود في الجملة.
For it there is existence and the non-existence of other than the opposite does not make the existence of the other opposite possible. And if not for the differentiation and the multiplicity necessary thereby among the non-existences, their implications and judgments would not differ, such as some being a necessary condition (malzūm) for another, or a necessary consequence (lāzim) of it, or equal, or opposite, to other than that which has no multiplicity. And some denied it because non-existents and non-existences are pure negation, having no reference at all. And everything that is distinct is either in the mind or in the external, because distinctness (tamayyuz) is a positive attribute (ṣifa thubūtiyya); it is necessary that the thing so described exists in some manner, and what is established in such a way is not non-existent. Therefore, no non-existent is distinct at all. His saying: 'Because non-existents etc.' i.e., in terms of their being non-existent, they are pure negation, i.e., absolute, having no reference to them, because reference requires identity (huwiyya) in the intellect which is incompatible with their being non-existent. This premise is self-evident, so it cannot be objected that your statement 'pure negation, no reference to them' is tantamount to saying they have no distinction, thus it would be circular. The form of the inference is: non-existents in terms of their being non-existent have no existence at all, and everything that is distinct exists in some manner. As for the minor premise, it is self-evident because non-existence contradicts subsistence (thubūt). As for the major premise, it is because everything distinct is characterized by the positive attribute of distinctness, and everything characterized by a positive attribute exists in some manner.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage presents the second opinion, which denies that non-existents are distinct. The proof is introduced: if non-existents were distinct, their necessary implications and judgments would differ (e.g., one being a cause, another an effect), but this difference could be due to their relations to privations, not to themselves. Then the outright denial is stated: non-existents and non-existences are pure negation with no reference; distinction requires a positive attribute, which necessitates existence. Therefore, no non-existent can be distinct. The commentator explains that the phrase 'pure negation' means there is no mental reference to them because reference requires identity, which contradicts non-existence. He then formalizes the argument into a syllogism: (1) Non-existents as such have no existence. (2) Everything distinct exists in some manner. The minor premise is self-evident; the major premise is because distinctness is a positive attribute, and anything with a positive attribute exists. Thus, the conclusion is that non-existents cannot be distinct.

Key Terms
المعدومات
العدمات
نفي صرف
الإشارة
الصفة الثبوتية
التميز
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument that 'non-existents are pure negation' is circular because it already assumes they have no distinction.
Correction: The argument is not circular; it is a valid syllogism. The minor premise (non-existents have no existence) is self-evident, and the major premise (everything distinct exists) is justified by the nature of distinction as a positive attribute. The commentator explicitly shows that it is not a petition principle.

Checkpoint
What is the major premise in the syllogism used to deny distinction of non-existents?
A) Non-existents are pure negation.
B) Everything that is distinct exists in some manner.
C) Non-existence contradicts subsistence.
D) Reference requires identity.

Key Takeaway
The second opinion denies distinction of non-existents by arguing that non-existents are pure negation with no reference, and distinction requires existence, so no non-existent can be distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Do you treat the desires of your heart—like a future you imagine—as if they have a reality of their own, when the lesson says they are pure negation with no reference? Will you now abandon that illusion and focus on what truly exists: your present actions in the sight of Allah?
?
When you fear the non-existence of something—like losing a loved one—are you mistakenly giving that potential non-existence a distinctness it does not have? Are you fearing something that has no reality? How does this change your approach to worry?
?
Have you ever argued with yourself about the 'nature' of a non-existent thing, treating it as if it had attributes? The lesson teaches that non-existents have no attributes at all. Will you stop such intellectual exercises and direct your mind only to what is real?

The Linguistic Meaning of Condition in the Condition Argument
Clarifies the linguistic meaning of 'condition' and resolves objections about the condition argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'condition' in the condition argument is interpreted linguistically to avoid a logical contradiction regarding the complete cause.

Core Idea
The condition argument works only if 'condition' means linguistic dependence, not a part of the cause.

The passage tackles a critical objection: if condition is part of the complete cause, then any part's non-existence would cause the effect to cease, contradicting the proof that only condition's non-existence does. The commentator explains that 'condition' here is used in its linguistic sense (that on which something depends in general), not the technical sense of a part of the complete cause. This resolution saves the argument and clarifies the meaning of condition in kalam debates.

Guided Path
1
Overview of the Objection
Read the passage to understand the main objection: if condition is a part of the complete cause, then non-existence of any part would cause the effect to cease, contradicting the proof.
The objection arises from a technical definition of condition.
2
The Two Interpretations
Identify the two possible meanings of 'condition': linguistic (general dependence) and technical (part of complete cause). The commentator argues that the proof uses the linguistic meaning.
This distinction is key to resolving the objection.
3
How the Resolution Works
See how the linguistic interpretation makes the proof valid: only the non-existence of the condition (in the linguistic sense) along with the rest of the cause leads to the effect's non-existence, while non-existence of other parts does not.
4
Reflection on Significance
Consider why this clarification matters for kalam debates: precise definition of terms prevents logical fallacies.
This shows the importance of terminological precision in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اقتصر على قوله فله وجود ولم يتعرض للوجود الذهني، ونفيه عن المعدوم الذي كلامنا فيه لكان أنسب بقوله : ( والحق فيه) أي في الخلاف في تمايز ثابتا المصطلح حتى يرد أن غير الشرط من أجزاء العلة التامة يوجب عدم المشروط، ولو أريد بقوله : لا يوجب عدم المشروط من حيث إنه مشروط يصح حمله على المعنى الاصطلاحي، فإن يوجب لا عدم سائر أجزاء العلة إنما يوجب عدمه من حيث إنه معلول لا من حيث أنه مشروط . قوله : (لم تختلف مقتضياتها فيه أن اللازم منه تمايز العدمات مطلقاً، والمطلوب تمايزها من حيث إنها معدومة فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الاختلاف بسبب إضافتها إلى ملكاتها ؟ . قوله : ( لأن المعدومات إلخ) قد مضى شرحه . قوله : ( ونفيه عن المعدوم التعرض لنفي الوجود الذهني في الصغرى مستفاد من التعرض له في الكبرى ليتكرر في الأوسط بأن يقال : المعدومات لا وجود لها في الذهن، والخارج وكل ما هو متميز موجود إما في الذهن أو في الخارج. قوله : ( أنسب ) لئلا يكون الاستدلال المذكور مشعراً بالتفصيل المذكور في قوله والحق . قوله : (فإن عدم غير الشرط لا يوجب عدم المشروط فإن قلت : عدم أي جزء كان من العلة التامة يوجب عدم المعلول، فما معنی هذا الكلام؟ قلت : المراد بالشرط ها هنا معناه اللغوي، وهو ما يتوقف عليه الشيء في الجملة، فيصح الكلام وإن حمل على معناه الاصطلاحي، فنقول مراده من قوله : فإن عدم الشرط يوجب عدم المشروط أن عدمه مع وجود باقي العلة التامة يوجبه، فحينئذ يكون معنى قوله : فإن عدم غير الشرط لا يوجب إلخ، أنه لا يوجب عدمه مع وجود أجزاء العلة التامة التي فرض وجودها في الصورة الأولى، وهذا حكم صحيح لأن تلك الأجزاء إذا فرض تحققها في الصورة الثانية أيضاً، كان غير الشرط الذي فرض عدمه فيها أجنبياً، ليس بجزء
And if he had only said 'for there is existence' and not mentioned mental existence, and its denial for the non-existent that is the subject of our discussion, it would be more appropriate to his saying: 'And the truth (al-ḥaqq) in it' i.e., in the dispute about differentiation, is established in the technical terminology such that it could be objected that other than the condition among the parts of the complete cause necessitates the non-existence of the conditioned. And if what is meant by his saying 'does not necessitate the non-existence of the conditioned' is in terms of it being conditioned, it is valid to interpret it according to the technical meaning, for indeed the non-existence of the other parts of the cause necessitates its non-existence only in terms of it being an effect, not in terms of it being conditioned. His saying: 'their implications would not differ' — in it (it is argued) that what necessarily follows is the differentiation of non-existences absolutely, but what is required is their differentiation in terms of being non-existent; so why is it not possible that this difference is due to their addition to their privations? His saying: 'Because non-existents etc.' its explanation has already passed. His saying: 'and its denial for the non-existent' — the mention of denial of mental existence in the minor premise is derived from mentioning it in the major premise so that it is repeated in the middle term, by saying: non-existents have no existence in the mind or the external, and everything that is distinct exists either in the mind or the external. His saying: 'more appropriate' so that the aforementioned inference does not hint at the detail mentioned in his saying 'and the truth'. His saying: 'for the non-existence of other than the condition does not necessitate the non-existence of the conditioned' — if you say: the non-existence of any part whatsoever of the complete cause necessitates the non-existence of the effect, so what is the meaning of this statement? I say: the intended meaning of 'condition' here is its linguistic meaning, which is that on which something depends in some way. Thus the statement is valid, even if interpreted according to its technical meaning. For we say: his intended meaning from his saying 'the non-existence of the condition necessitates the non-existence of the conditioned' is that its non-existence along with the existence of the rest of the complete cause necessitates it. Therefore, the meaning of his saying 'the non-existence of other than the condition does not necessitate' etc. is that it does not necessitate its non-existence along with the existence of the parts of the complete cause that were assumed to exist in the first scenario. This is a correct judgment because those parts, if they are assumed to be realized in the second scenario as well, then the non-condition whose non-existence was assumed in it is extraneous, not a part.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage addresses interpretative issues in the first opinion's proof. The commentator first notes that it would have been better if the argument had not mentioned mental existence, to avoid confusion. He discusses the meaning of 'condition' (shart): if taken technically (as part of the complete cause), then the non-existence of any part of the complete cause necessitates the non-existence of the effect, which would contradict the proof's claim that only the condition's non-existence does so. The resolution is that 'condition' here is used in the linguistic sense (what something depends on in general), not the technical sense. Alternatively, if the technical sense is intended, the proof can be reinterpreted: the non-existence of the condition along with the existence of the other parts necessitates the non-existence of the conditioned, whereas the non-existence of a non-condition (an extraneous part) does not. The commentator also addresses the objection that the difference in implications among non-existences could be due to their relations to privations, not to their own natures. He then explains that the denial of mental existence in the minor premise is clarified by the logical structure. Finally, he justifies the linguistic interpretation of 'condition' to maintain the proof's validity.

Key Terms
الشرط (condition)
العلة التامة (complete cause)
المعنى اللغوي (linguistic meaning)
المعنى الاصطلاحي (technical meaning)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'condition' in the proof refers to the technical term of a part of the complete cause, leading to an apparent contradiction.
Correction: It actually refers to the linguistic meaning of general dependence, which allows the proof to hold that only the non-existence of this condition (not other parts) necessitates the non-existence of the conditioned.

Checkpoint
How is the term 'condition' (shart) interpreted in the first opinion's proof to avoid the objection regarding the complete cause?
A) It is interpreted technically as a part of the complete cause.
B) It is interpreted linguistically as that on which something depends in general.
C) It is interpreted as the opposite of the effect.
D) It is considered irrelevant to the argument.

Key Takeaway
The resolution of the condition argument relies on understanding 'condition' in its broad linguistic sense, not as a technical part of the cause.

?
